Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Wiping Forehead in Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wiping forehead during prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios of wiping the forehead during prayer and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
Wiping the forehead during prayer is disliked unless needed; after prayer it is recommended.

This passage addresses a seemingly small action—wiping the forehead—yet illustrates how Hanafi jurists carefully differentiate rulings based on timing and necessity. We will explore four scenarios and an intra-Hanafi difference between two prominent scholars, al-Sarakhsi and al-Halwani. By the end, you will understand not only the practical ruling but also how the school reconciles textual variations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the four timings for wiping the forehead: (1) after salam, (2) after completing prayer actions but before salam, (3) after the last sajdah but before tashahhud, (4) during the prayer itself.
These scenarios are derived from Ibn Amir Hajj's summary.
2
Note the ruling for each scenario
For each, state whether it is disliked (makruh), permissible (mubah), or recommended (mustahab). The passage provides specific rulings based on need and timing.
After salam is mustahab; before salam is acceptable; during prayer is generally makruh unless there is a need.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Focus on scenario three: wiping after the last sajdah but before tashahhud. Note that al-Sarakhsi permits it, while al-Halwani reports textual disagreement. Understand the basis of each view.
The difference stems from varying weight given to hadiths about fidgeting versus the permissibility of minor actions.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
Identify the relied-upon ruling: wiping during prayer is disliked unless there is a need. Note that this is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah and is confirmed by later authorities like al-Tahtawi.
The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah, as stated in al-Tuhfah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع: كره مسح جبهته من نحو تراب كحشيش، أو عرق في خلالها إلا لحاجة تدعو إلى ذلك فإن دعت إليه الحاجة بأن ضره أو شغله عن الخشوع فلا كراهة، وأما بعد السلام فلا يكره لما روى ابن السني في كتابه عن أنس قال: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا قضى صلاته جبهته بيده اليمنى، ثم قال : أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله الرحمن الرحيم اللهم أذهب عني الهم والحزن . قال المحقق ابن أمير حاج حاصل هذه المسألة أربعة وجوه . أحدها أن من العرق، أو التراب بعد السلام، فذلك مستحب لأنه خرج من الصلاة وفيه إزالة الأذى عن نفسه. الثاني أن : يمسح بعد الفراغ من أعمال الصلاة قبل السلام قال في البدائع : لا بأس به بالإجماع لأنه لو قطع الصلاة في هذه الحالة لا يكره فلأن لا يكره إدخال فعل قليل أولى . الثالث أن يمسح بعد ما رفع رأسه من السجدة الأخيرة قبل أن يقعد قدر التشهد، فقال السرخسي : لا بأس به وقال الحلوني : فيه اختلاف ألفاظ الكتب ففي بعضها أكره ذلك وفي بعضها لا أكره ذلك، ولكل دليل من السنة الرابع أن يمسح في خلال الصلاة أهـ، وظاهر الرواية كما في التحفة أنه يكره وهو الصحيح.
Branches: It is disliked to wipe his forehead from something like dust, grass, or sweat during it (prayer) unless there is a need that calls for it; if the need calls for it, such as it harms him or distracts him from khushu', then it is not disliked. As for after the salam, it is not disliked due to what Ibn al-Sunni narrated in his book from Anas: 'When the Messenger of Allah finished his prayer, he would wipe his forehead with his right hand, then say: I bear witness that there is no god but Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. O Allah, remove from me worry and grief.' The scholar Ibn Amir Hajj said: The summary of this issue is four aspects. The first: that he wipes after the salam, and that is mustahab because he has left the prayer and it removes harm from himself. The second: that he wipes after completing the actions of the prayer before the salam. Al-Sarakhsi said: There is no harm in it, and al-Halwani said: There is disagreement in the wording of the books; in some I dislike that, and in some I do not dislike it, and each has evidence from the Sunnah. The fourth: that he wipes during the prayer. The apparent narration as in al-Tuhfah is that it is disliked, and that is the correct view.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a furu' (branch) on the ruling of wiping the forehead during prayer. It begins by stating that it is disliked (makruh) to wipe dust, grass, or sweat from the forehead during prayer unless there is a need (e.g., it harms or distracts from khushu'). After prayer (after salam), it is not disliked; in fact, it is mustahab based on a hadith from Anas that the Prophet would wipe his forehead after prayer. The commentator, Ibn Amir Hajj, summarizes four scenarios: (1) after salam - mustahab; (2) after completing prayer actions but before salam - no harm (la ba'sa) according to consensus in al-Bada'i'; (3) after raising head from the last sajdah but before sitting for tashahhud - a dispute between al-Sarakhsi (permissible) and al-Halwani (disliked in some texts); (4) during the prayer (e.g., in the middle) - disliked according to the apparent narration and the correct view. This illustrates an intra-Hanafi difference regarding timing.

Key Terms
مسح الجبهة (mash al-jabhah): Wiping the forehead to remove sweat, dust, etc.
أبث (abath): Fidgeting or unnecessary movement during prayer.
خشوع (khushu'): Humility and concentration in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wiping the forehead after completing the prayer (after salam)?
A) It is makruh (disliked).
B) It is mustahab (recommended).
C) It is haram (prohibited).
D) It is mubah (permissible).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes four timings for wiping the forehead during prayer, with the general rule that it is disliked unless a need exists, and recommended after prayer.

Disliked Actions in Prayer (Makruhat al-Salah) with Detailed Commentary
Commentary on five makruhat in prayer with proofs and intra-madhhab analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five disliked actions in prayer according to the Hanafi school, explain their proofs, and distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi for each.

Core Idea
Small actions during prayer break its perfection—some are prohibitedly disliked, others lightly disliked.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya refines the rulings on five actions that detract from the perfection of salah. While the prayer remains valid, these actions reduce its reward or may require compensation. Understanding the gradation between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi is essential for the practicing Hanafi.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked action and its evidence
List the five actions: cracking knuckles, interlocking fingers, placing hands on hips, wiping forehead, and turning the neck. For each, note the primary evidence cited (hadith or athar) and the source.
Cracking: hadith 'لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت في الصلاة'; Interlocking: Ibn Umar's statement and hadith; Hands on hips: prohibition; Wiping: hadith from Ibn al-Sunni; Turning: hadith from Aisha.
2
Classify the hukm level for each action
Determine whether each is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) or makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). Consider the context: during prayer vs. outside, with or without need.
Cracking and interlocking are tahrimi according to the stronger view; hands on hips is also tahrimi; wiping during prayer is tanzihi unless a need; turning neck is also disliked depending on type.
3
Trace the four scenarios for wiping the forehead
Analyze the ikhtilaf regarding wiping after salam (mustahabb), after completing actions before salam (no harm), after last prostration before tashahhud (dispute between Sarakhsi and Halwani), and during prayer (makruh).
The mufta bihi is that it is makruh during the prayer, including the period before tashahhud.
4
Distinguish the three types of turning the neck
Understand the three levels: disliked (turning neck), permissible (looking with side of eyes), invalidating (turning chest from qibla during a pillar).
The passage emphasizes that turning chest invalidates if it involves facing away during a rukn; otherwise it does not.
5
Apply the rulings to practical scenarios
Given a scenario, state the hukm and explain why, referencing the appropriate category and evidence.
For example, a person walking to the mosque interlocks fingers: makruh tahrimi because considered in state of prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرة وهو غمزها أو مذها حتى تصوت لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت في الصلاة (وتشبيكها) لقول ابن عمر فيه تلك صلاة المغضوب عليهم والتخصر) لأنه نهى عنه في قوله : ( ولأن تمسك عنها الخ هذا يدل على أن الترك أولى وصرح به في البدائع، والنهاية قال في البحر : لأنه كان يمكنه التسوية قبل الشروع فكان مقصراً في تركه اهـ. قوله: (سود الحدق) كناية عن العظم، وغلاء القيم . مسح يمسح جبهته

فروع: کره مسح جبهته من نحو تراب كحشيش، أو عرق في خلالها إلا لحاجة تدعو إلى ذلك فإن دعت إليه الحاجة بأن ضره أو شغله عن الخشوع فلا كراهة، وأما بعد السلام فلا يكره لما روى ابن السني في كتابه عن أنس قال: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا قضى صلاته جبهته بيده اليمنى، ثم قال : أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله الرحمن الرحيم اللهم أذهب عني الهم والحزن . قال المحقق ابن أمير :حاج حاصل هذه المسألة أربعة وجوه . أحدها أن من العرق، أو التراب بعد السلام، فذلك مستحب لأنه خرج من الصلاة وفيه إزالة الأذى عن نفسه. الثاني أن : يسمح بعد الفراغ من أعمال الصلاة قبل السلام قال في البدائع : لا بأس به بالإجماع لأنه لو قطع الصلاة في هذه الحالة لا يكره فلأن لا يكره إدخال فعل قليل أولى . الثالث أن يمسح بعد ما رفع رأسه من السجدة الأخيرة قبل أن يقعد قدر التشهد، فقال السرخسي : لا بأس به وقال الحلوني : فيه اختلاف ألفاظ الكتب ففي بعضها أكره ذلك وفي بعضها لا أكره ذلك، ولكل دليل من السنة الرابع أن يمسح في خلال الصلاة أهـ، وظاهر الرواية كما في التحفة أنه يكره وهو الصحيح. قوله : (لا تفرقع الخ) هذا يفيد التحريم، وألحق في المجتبى منتظر الصلاة، والماشي إليها بمن فيها، وأما خارج الصلاة ففي القهستاني، وتكره خارج الصلاة عند كثرين ،اهـ وعلله في المجتبى كما في البحر بأنها من الشيطان لكن قال : لما لم يكن فيها خارجها فهي لم تكن تحريمية ،اهـ وعلل في البرهان الكراهة بأنه نوع من وقال : الضاحك في الصلاة والملتفت والمفرقع أصابعه سواء» يعني في الإثم كذا في مجمع الروايات، وإنما كره لأنه عمل قوم لوط فيكره التشبه بهم، قال صلى الله عليه وسلم لعلي : «أني أحب لك ما أحب لنفسي لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت تصلي، كذا في المستصفى.

العبث

قوله : (وتشيبكها ولو حال السعي إلى الصلاة لما روى أحمد، وأبو داود، وغيرهما مرفوعاً، إذا توضأ أحدكم فأحسن وضواه، ثم خرج عامداً إلى المسجد، فلا يشبك بين يديه فإنه في صلاة، وإذا كان منتظراً لها بالأولى، والذي يظهر أنها أيضاً تحريمية للنهي المذكور كما في البحر، وأما إذا انصرف من الصلاة فلا بأس به وحكمة النهي عن التشبيك أنه من كما في الحديث، وأنه يجلب النوم، وهو من مظان الحدث، وأن صورة التشبيك تشبه صورة الاختلاف كما نبه عليه في حديث ابن عمر فكره ذلك لما هو في حكم الصلاة حتى لا يقع في الصلاة وهو

الله عنها سألت أن يضع يده على خاصرته وهو أشهر وأصح تأويلاتها لما فيه من ترك سنة أخذ اليدين والتشبه بالجبابرة والالتفات بعنقه) لا بعينه لقول عائشة رضي رسول الله له عن التفات الرجل في الصلاة فقال : هو اختلاس يختلسه الشيطان . من العبد» رواه البخاري وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا يزال الله مقبلاً على العبد وهو في صلاته ما لم يلتفت

صلاة المنهي عنه. قوله : (وهو) أن يضع يده على خاصرته وهي ما بين عظم رأس الورك، وأسفل الأضلاع، أفاده في القاموس وفي المصباح الإختصار، والتخصر في الصلاة، وضع اليد على الخصر، وهو وسط الإنسان، وهو المستدق فوق الوركين اهـ وقيل هو أن يتكيء على عصا في الصلاة، وتسمى المخصرة بكسر الميم ، ومنه قوله لابن أنيس وقد أعطاه عصا تخصر بها : فإن المتخصريينن في الجنة كما في التبيين، ولا شك في كراهة الإتكاء في الفرض لغير ضرورة كما صرحوا به لا في النفل مطلقاً على الأصح كما في المجتبى وقيل : هو أن لا. حدود الصلاة، فإن لزم منه ترك واجب كره تحريماً، وإن أخل بسنة كره تنزيهاً وقيل : أن يختصر القراءة فإن أخل بواجب كره تحريماً، وإلا فلا قال في النهر : وهذه التأويلات لييس في اللفظ ما يمنع واحداً منها إلا أن الأنسب هو الأول اهـ.

يتم المسجد

قوله: (وهو أشهر وأصح تأويلاتها وبه قال الجمهور من أهل اللغة والحديث والفقه . قوله: (لما فيه الخ) أي فالكراهة لها سببان سبب يقتضي كراهة التنزيه، وسبب يقتضي كراهة التحريم. قال في البحر والذي يظهر أنها تحريمية للنهي المذكور كذا في الشرح. قوله : والإلتفات بعنقه لا بعينه الإلتفات ثلاثة أنواع مكروه، وهو ما ذكر، ومباح وهو أن ينظر بمؤخر عينيه يمنة ويسرة من غير أن يلوي عنقه ومبطل وهو أن يحول صدره عن القبلة إذا وقف قدر أداء ركن مستدبراً كما بحثه في البحر، وهذا إذا كان من غير عذر أما به فلا لتصريحهم بأنه لو ظن أنه أحدث فاستدبر القبلة، ثم علم أنه لم يحدث ولم يخرج من لا تبطل، وفي الشرح والأولى ترك النوع الثاني لأنه ينافي الأدب لغير حاجة والظاهر أن فعله إياه كان لحاجة تفقد أحوال المقتدين مع ما فيه من بيان الجواز، وإلا فهو صلى الله عليه وسلم كان خلفه كما ينظر من أمامه كما في الصحيحين قوله: (عن التفات الرجل في ومثله المرأة، والخنثى في هذا الحكم قوله : (هو اختلاس) أي اختطاف بسرعة، والمراد والله أعلم أن الشيطان يشغله حتى يأخذ بعضاً من صلاته فينقص ثواب ذلك المأخوذ، ولما كان ذلك على سبيل الغرة والغفلة مع تمكن الآخذ (۱) ورغبة المأخوذ منه في غير ذلك أطلق عليه الاختلاس، قوله : مقبلاً على العبد أي بمزيد رحمته وإحسانه .

(۱) قوله ورغبة في نسخة ورعنة ولعله الأوفق إلا أن مقتضى صنيع القاموس والصحاح أن يقال رعن التحريك لا رعنة فليحرر اهـ مصححه .
Once, and it is pressing them or rubbing them until they sound, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer.' (And interlocking them) because of Ibn Umar's saying: 'That is the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry.' (And placing hands on hips) because he forbade it in his saying: 'And because refraining from it is better...' This indicates that leaving it is preferable, and it is stated explicitly in al-Bada'i' and al-Nihaya. He said in al-Bahr: 'Because he could have straightened them before starting, so he was negligent in leaving it.' His saying: 'Blackening the eyes' is a metaphor for greatness and high value. Wiping: He wipes his forehead. Branches: It is disliked to wipe his forehead from something like dust, grass, or sweat during it unless a need calls for that. If need arises because it harms him or distracts him from khushu', then there is no dislike. After the salam, it is not disliked because of what Ibn al-Sunni narrated in his book from Anas: 'When the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, finished his prayer, he would wipe his forehead with his right hand, then say: I bear witness that there is no god but Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. O Allah, remove from me worry and grief.' The investigator Ibn Amir said: The summary of this issue has four aspects. First: wiping sweat or dust after salam is mustahabb because he has left the prayer and it removes harm from himself. Second: wiping after completing the actions of prayer before salam: al-Bada'i' says there is no harm by consensus because if he cut the prayer in this state it is not disliked, so it is more appropriate that introducing a small action is not disliked. Third: wiping after raising his head from the last prostration before sitting for the duration of tashahhud: al-Sarakhsi said no harm, al-Halwani said there is a difference in the wording of the books, some say I dislike that, some say I do not dislike that, and each has a proof from the Sunnah. Fourth: wiping during the prayer. End. The apparent riwaya as in al-Tuhfa is that it is disliked, and that is correct. His saying: 'Do not crack' etc. indicates prohibition. In al-Mujtaba, the one waiting for prayer and the one walking to it are attached to the one in it. As for outside prayer, in al-Qahistani, it is disliked outside prayer according to many. End. He explained in al-Mujtaba as in al-Bahr that it is from the shaytan, but he said: 'Since it is not [in prayer] outside it, it is not prohibitive.' End. And in al-Burhan, he explained the dislike as a type of ... And he said: 'The one who laughs in prayer, the one who turns, and the one who cracks his fingers are equal' meaning in sin, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. It is disliked because it is the action of the people of Lut, so it is disliked to imitate them. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Ali: 'I love for you what I love for myself: do not crack your fingers while you are praying' as in al-Mustasfa. Al-Abath. His saying: 'And interlocking them even when walking to prayer' because of what Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and others narrated marfu'an: 'When one of you performs wudu well, then goes out intending the mosque, let him not interlock his fingers because he is in prayer, and when he is waiting for it even more so.' It appears that it is also prohibitive because of the mentioned prohibition as in al-Bahr. After finishing prayer, there is no harm. The wisdom of the prohibition of interlocking is that it is from [the shaytan] as in the hadith, and it brings sleep, which is a place of hadath, and the form of interlocking resembles the form of difference as pointed out in the hadith of Ibn Umar, so it is disliked because he is in the state of prayer, so that he does not fall into the prayer and he is... Allah from her asked that he place his hand on his waist, which is the most famous and correct interpretation, because it involves abandoning the sunnah of taking the hands and imitating the tyrants. And turning the neck, not the eyes, because of the saying of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'The Messenger of Allah was asked about a man turning in prayer, so he said: It is a snatching that the shaytan snatches from the servant.' Narrated by al-Bukhari. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah continues to turn to His servant while he is in his prayer as long as he does not turn away.' The prayer of the prohibited. His saying: (It) is that he places his hand on his waist (khasira), which is between the head of the hip bone and the lower ribs, as stated in al-Qamus and al-Misbah: al-ikhtisar and al-takhassur in prayer is placing the hand on the waist, which is the middle of a person, the part above the hips. End. It is also said: It is to lean on a stick in prayer, called al-mikhsara. From it is his saying to Ibn Unays when he gave him a stick to lean on: 'Indeed, those who lean on sticks in Paradise' as in al-Tabyin. There is no doubt about the dislike of leaning in fard without necessity as they stated, but not in nafl absolutely according to the sahih as in al-Mujtaba. It is also said: It is that... limits of the prayer, for if it leads to abandoning a wajib, it is makruh tahrimi, and if it neglects a sunnah, it is makruh tanzihi. It is also said: To shorten the recitation, for if it leads to abandoning a wajib, it is makruh tahrimi, otherwise no. He said in al-Nahr: 'These interpretations have nothing in the wording to prevent any of them, but the most appropriate is the first.' End. He completes the mosque. His saying: (It is the most famous and correct of its interpretations) and the majority of linguists, hadith scholars, and jurists said it. His saying: (Because of what it contains) i.e., the dislike has two causes: a cause that necessitates makruh tanzihi and a cause that necessitates makruh tahrimi. He said in al-Bahr: 'It appears that it is tahrimi because of the mentioned prohibition' as in the sharh. His saying: And turning the neck, not the eyes: turning is of three types: disliked, which is what was mentioned; permissible, which is to look with the side of his eyes right and left without twisting his neck; invalidating, which is to turn his chest away from the qibla if he stands for the duration of performing a pillar facing away, as researched in al-Bahr. This is if without excuse, but with excuse no, because of their explicit statement that if he thought he had had a hadath and faced away from the qibla, then knew he had not had a hadath and had not exited, it does not invalidate. In the sharh, it is better to leave the second type because it contradicts good manners without need, and it appears that his action of it was for a need to check the conditions of the followers, along with what it contains of indicating permissibility. Otherwise, he, peace be upon him, was behind him as he looked from in front of him as in the two Sahihs. His saying: (About a man turning in prayer) and likewise a woman and a khuntha in this ruling. His saying: (It is a snatching) i.e., a swift seizure, and the meaning, Allah knows best, is that the shaytan distracts him until he takes some of his prayer, decreasing the reward of that taken part. Since that is by way of surprise and heedlessness, with the taker being able and the one taken from desiring otherwise, it is called snatching. His saying: 'Turning to the servant' means with abundant mercy and benevolence. (1) His saying 'desire' in some copies is 'rawna' and perhaps it is more suitable, except that the usage of al-Qamus and al-Sihah requires that it be said 'ra'ana' with a fat-ha, not 'ra'na'? Let it be checked. End. The corrector.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on the Maraqi al-Falah, explaining five disliked actions (makruhat) in prayer. The author begins by defining cracking knuckles (tafriq al-asabi') as pressing or rubbing until sound is produced, citing the hadith 'Do not crack your fingers while in prayer.' This indicates tahrim (prohibition) according to the text, though some scholars differentiate. The same ruling applies to one waiting for prayer or walking to it, while outside prayer it is disliked (makruh) but not tahrimi. Next, interlocking fingers (tashbik) is disliked based on the hadith 'Do not interlock your fingers while going to the mosque' and the report from Ibn Umar. The ruling is tahrimi (prohibitive dislike) during prayer and while proceeding to it; after prayer it is permissible. Placing hands on hips (takhassur) is disliked because it involves imitating tyrants and abandoning the sunnah of hand placement. Multiple interpretations exist: placing hand on the waist, leaning on a stick, or shortening recitation. The first is the most famous. Regarding wiping the forehead (mash al-jabhah), the passage details four scenarios: (1) after salam – mustahabb, (2) after completing prayer actions but before salam – no problem, (3) after raising from last prostration before tashahhud – ikhtilaf between Sarakhsi (no harm) and Halwani (dislike), (4) during prayer – makruh according to the sahih. The last is the relied-upon position. Finally, turning the neck (iltifat) is of three types: disliked (turning neck), permissible (looking with side of eyes), and invalidating (turning chest from qibla during a pillar). The hadith from Aisha states 'It is a snatching from the shaytan.' The overall lesson is that these actions detract from the perfection of prayer; some are more severe than others.

Key Terms
تفرقع الأصابع (tafriq al-asabi'): Pressing or rubbing the fingers until they produce a cracking sound, specifically during prayer
تشبيك الأصابع (tashbik al-asabi'): Interlocking the fingers of one hand with the other, considered disliked during prayer and when walking to the mosque
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing the hand on the hip (khasira) during prayer, which is disliked. Other interpretations include leaning on a stick or shortening recitation.
مسح الجبهة (mash al-jabhah): Wiping the forehead to remove sweat, dust, etc. The ruling depends on when it is done: mustahabb after salam, makruh during prayer.
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the neck (not the eyes) away from the qibla during prayer. Three types: disliked (turning neck), permissible (looking with side of eyes), invalidating (turning chest from qibla).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on interlocking fingers (tashbik) while walking to the mosque?
A) Permissible (mubah) because one is not yet in the prayer
B) Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because one is considered in the state of prayer
C) Makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) only
D) Haram (forbidden) absolutely

Key Takeaway
While the prayer remains valid, these disliked actions diminish its perfection and reward; the Hanafi school carefully distinguishes degrees of disapproval based on the strength of the evidence.

Ruling on Placing Hand on Hip (Takhassur) in Prayer
Explains ruling and interpretations of takhassur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of takhassur in Hanafi fiqh, explain the multiple interpretations, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Takhassur is makruh tahrimi because it involves turning away and imitating tyrants.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi examines the disliked act of placing one's hand on the hip during prayer (takhassur). The author presents multiple interpretations of the term and clarifies that the most appropriate view—placing the hand on the waist—is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). The ruling is derived from the hadith prohibiting turning in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what takhassur is and what the Hanafi ruling is (makruh tanzih or tahrimi) according to the text.
The text states the most famous interpretation is placing hand on hip.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary proof cited: the hadith of Aisha about turning in prayer.
Hadith: 'It is a snatch that the devil snatches from the servant.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the ruling. The prohibition of turning implies that any associated act (like hand on hip) is also prohibited.
The hadith labels turning as devil's snatching; takhassur involves turning the neck.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
List the different interpretations of takhassur (hand on hip, leaning on stick, etc.) and their different rulings.
Some interpretations lead to makruh tanzih, others to tahrimi.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Identify which interpretation the commentator favors and why, and what the mufta bihi position is.
Al-Nahr says the first interpretation is most appropriate; Al-Bahr concludes makruh tahrimi.

Book of Prayer. Prayer and it is [the disliked action] that he places his hand on his hip, and it is the most famous and correct of its interpretations because it involves abandoning the sunnah of taking the hands and imitating tyrants, and turning the neck, not the eyes, due to the saying of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, who asked: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade turning in prayer and said: It is a snatch that the devil snatches from the servant.' Narrated by Bukhari. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah continues to turn to the servant while he is in his prayer as long as he does not turn away.' [Then] the commentator's statement: '(And it is) that he places his hand on his hip, which is the area between the head bone of the hip and the lower ribs.' Explained in al-Qamus and al-Misbah: 'Takhassur in prayer is placing the hand on the waist, the middle of the person, the part above the hips.' And it is said: it is to lean on a stick in prayer, called al-mikhsarah; from it is his saying to Ibn Unays when he gave him a stick to lean on: 'Those who lean on sticks in Paradise' as in al-Tabyin. There is no doubt about the dislike of leaning in obligatory prayer without necessity as they stated, but not in supererogatory prayer absolutely according to the most correct view as in al-Mujtaba. And it is said: it is that he exceeds the limits of prayer, so if it necessitates abandoning an obligatory act it is prohibitively disliked, and if it omits a sunnah it is slightly disliked. And it is said: that he shortens the recitation; if he omits an obligatory act it is prohibitively disliked, otherwise not. Al-Nahr said: 'These interpretations, there is nothing in the wording to prevent any one of them, but the most appropriate is the first.' 'He completes the mosque.' The commentator's statement: 'And it is the most famous and correct of its interpretations, and the majority of linguists, hadith scholars, and jurists adhere to it.' His statement: 'Because it involves, etc.' meaning the dislike has two causes: a cause that necessitates slight dislike and a cause that necessitates prohibitive dislike. Al-Bahr said: 'What appears is that it is prohibitive due to the mentioned prohibition, as in the Sharh.'

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi discusses the ruling of placing one's hand on the hip during prayer (takhassur). The author first quotes Aisha's hadith about turning in prayer, then defines takhassur lexically and technically. Several interpretations are given: placing hand on waist, leaning on a staff, going beyond prayer boundaries, or shortening recitation. Each interpretation has a different ruling level of dislike (tanzihi or tahrimi). The commentator favors the first interpretation as the most famous and correct, and notes that the majority of scholars agree. The passage then discusses that the dislike may have two causes: one leading to slight dislike and one to prohibitive dislike, with Al-Bahr opining that it is prohibitive due to the explicit prohibition.

Key Terms
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing one's hand on the hip during prayer, derived from 'khisr' (waist).
كراهة (karaha): Dislike, which can be tanzih (slightly disliked) or tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).

The Three Types of Turning in Prayer
Classifies turning in prayer into three types with their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of turning in prayer and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
Turning the neck is prohibitively disliked; turning the chest away invalidates prayer.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies a common point of confusion: not all turning during prayer is the same. The author classifies three types, each with a distinct ruling, and supports the prohibition with a hadith from Bukhari. Understanding these nuances helps you avoid invalidating your prayer unnecessarily while maintaining proper adab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of iltifat
Read the text and list the three types: turning the neck, looking with the eyes, and turning the chest away. For each, note the ruling mentioned.
2
Locate the dalil for the prohibition
The text cites the hadith: 'It is a snatch that the devil snatches from the servant.' Identify the source (Bukhari) and the phrase used.
The hadith is ahad (single-chain), but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The term 'snatch' implies the devil steals part of the reward, indicating the act is disliked (makruh) but not invalidating.
Hanafis interpret the snatch as a partial loss, not total annulment.
4
Note the exception for excuses
The text says turning the chest away does not invalidate if done due to an excuse (e.g., thinking wudu broke). Recognize that the ruling is flexible.
This exception is derived from the principle that necessity overrides prohibition.
5
Summarize the practical ruling
State the mufta bihi: turning the neck is makruh tahrimi, looking with eyes is mubah but better avoided, turning chest away for a pillar is mubtil unless excused.

His statement: 'And turning the neck, not the eyes. Turning is of three types: disliked, which is what was mentioned; permissible, which is to look with the back of his eyes to the right and left without twisting his neck; and invalidating, which is to turn his chest away from the qiblah for the duration of performing a pillar while turning his back, as discussed in al-Bahr. This is if without an excuse; but if there is an excuse, then not, because they explicitly state that if he thought he had broken wudu and turned away from the qiblah, then learned that he had not broken wudu and did not exit [the prayer], it is not invalidated. In the Sharh, it is better to avoid the second type because it contradicts good manners without need, and apparently the Prophet did it only out of need to check the conditions of the followers, along with indicating its permissibility; otherwise, he used to see what was behind him as he saw what was in front, as in the Sahihayn. Commentary
This passage classifies turning (iltifat) during prayer into three types: (1) Makruh (disliked): turning the neck; (2) Mubah (permissible): looking sideways with only the eyes; (3) Mubtil (invalidating): turning the chest away from the qiblah for the duration of a pillar with one's back to it. The commentary cites Al-Bahr for the third type and notes that if there is an excuse (like thinking one had broken wudu), it does not invalidate. It also advises avoiding the second type as against good manners, unless justified by need. The Prophet's own turning was for a need. The hadith describes turning as a 'snatch' by the devil, meaning the devil steals part of the prayer's reward.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the body or head away from the qiblah during prayer.
اختلاس (ikhtilas): A snatch or swift seizure; used for the devil's act of stealing reward from prayer.

Key Takeaway
Turning in prayer is not monolithic: turning the neck is prohibitively disliked, turning the eyes is permissible, and turning the chest away for a pillar invalidates the prayer.

Spitting During Prayer: Ruling and Etiquette
Details the disliked nature of spitting and proper methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on spitting during prayer, identify the proper direction based on hadith, and explain the role of repentance in expiating the sin of spitting in the mosque.

Core Idea
Spitting in the mosque is a sin; expiation requires burial and repentance, and the proper direction during prayer is left or under the foot.

In the Hanafi school, spitting during prayer is not merely a hygiene issue—it carries a legal ruling. The Prophet ﷺ taught us where to direct our saliva when in prayer, emphasizing that we are in the presence of Allah. This lesson unpacks the hadith, the ruling of makruh tahrimi for certain directions, and the unique exception for the Prophet's mosque. By understanding these details, you'll grasp how the Hanafi school balances textual evidence with practical considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Spitting during prayer is the act under scrutiny. The text states it is disliked (makruh) in front or to the right, and a sin (khati'ah) inside the mosque. Distinguish between outside and inside the mosque.
The ruling outside is makruh tanzihi? Actually it's makruh tahrimi due to sin.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Bukhari and Muslim is cited: 'When one of you stands to pray, let him not spit in front of him...' This is the primary proof.
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'let him spit to his left or under his foot' indicates a recommended direction, while the prohibition 'let him not spit' indicates dislike. The Hanafi usul principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub? Here it is for guidance, not obligation.
The prohibition is clear, so spitting in front or right is at least makruh.
4
Analyze the commentary's refinements
Al-Tahtawi addresses the apparent contradiction of the two angels (singular vs plural) and the exception for the Prophet's mosque. He explains that spitting to the left hits the devil, and in the Rawdah, one may spit to the right.
This is critical refinement of the ruling based on context.
5
Resolve the objection about expiation
The text raises: if spitting in the mosque is a sin, how can burial alone expiate? The answer: burial is only with repentance, as implied by calling it a sin.
This shows the importance of intention and repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن التفت انصرف عنه ويكره أن يرمي بزاقه إلا أن يضطر فيأخذه بثوبه أو يلقيه تحت رجله اليسرى إذا صلى خارج المسجد لما في البخاري أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال : إذا قام أحدكم إلى الصلاة فلا يبصق أمامه فإنما يناجي الله تعالى ما دام في مصلاه، ولا عن يمينه فإن عن يمينه ملكين وليبصق عن يساره أو تحت قدمه وفي رواية أو تحت قدمه اليسرى وفي الصحيحين البزاق في المسجد خطيئة وكفارتها دفنها قوله : (انصرف عنه أي منع عنه ذلك الإحسان قوله : ( ويكره أن يرمي بزاقه البزاق كغراب ماء الفم إذا خرج منه، وما دام فيه فهو ريق فتسميته بزاقاً باعتبار المال، ويقال : بالصاد والسين المهملتين. قوله : إذا قام أحدكم إلى (الصلاة ظاهره أنه يكره حال القيام إليها قبل الدخول فيها إلحاقاً له بها قوله: (فإنما يناجي الله) أي يتحدث معه، ويتكلم بكلامه، وهذا على سبيل التمثيل لأن شأن المناجي أن يواجه من يناجيه، فلا يقابله بما يخل بالأدب لا سيما إذا كان عظيماً فيمثل المصلي حاله في حال صلاته بحال من يناجي عظيماً مواجهاً له، فلا يأتي بما فيه سوء الأدب قوله : فإن عن يمينه ملكين الحديث المتفق عليه ملكاً بالإفراد، واستشكل بأن في اليسار أيضاً ملكاً، وأجيب بأنه ورد في حديث أبي أمامة فإنه يقوم بين يدي الله تعالى، وملك عن يمينه وقرينه عن يساره أي فلعل المصلي إذا تقل عن يساره يقع على قرينه، وهو الشيطان، ولا يصيب الملك منه شيء كما في العيني على البخاري، وفي شرح عن الحافظ ابن حجر، واستثنى بعضهم من بالمسجد النبوي مستقبلاً القبلة فإن بصاقه عن يمينه أولى لأنه عن يساره .اهـ. قال وهو وجه وجيه كما لو كان على يساره جماعة ولم يتمكن منه تحت قدمه، فإن الظاهر حينئذ أنه عن اليمين أولى اهـ قلت: لا سيما إذا كان المصلي في الروضة . قوله : ( وفي الصحيحين (الخ أورد أنه يدل على جواز البزاق في المسجد لأنه لو كان معصية لم يكفر بالدفن وحده بل بالتوبة أجيب بأن التوبة عن كل ذنب لما كانت معلومة الوجوب سكت عنها، فيكون معنى قوله : وكفارتها دفنها أي مع التوبة بدليل تسميتها خطيئة قال ابن أمير حاج.
If he turns away, it (the mercy) departs from him. And it is disliked to spit except if compelled, then he should take it in his garment or throw it under his left foot if he prays outside the mosque, because in Bukhari that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'When one of you stands up to pray, he should not spit in front of him, for he is communing with Allah while in his prayer place; nor to his right, for on his right are two angels; let him spit to his left or under his foot.' In another narration: 'or under his left foot.' In the Sahihayn: 'Spitting in the mosque is a sin, and its expiation is to bury it.' The commentator's statement: 'He turned away from him' meaning that goodness was withheld from him. His statement: 'And it is disliked to spit' - 'bazag' is saliva when it leaves the mouth; while inside it is 'reeq'; it is called 'bazag' in view of the liquid; it is pronounced with 'sad' or 'seen'. His statement: 'When one of you stands to pray' - apparently it is disliked while standing up to it before entering it, by analogy. His statement: 'He is communing with Allah' i.e., speaking with Him, talking with His speech; this is figurative because a person communing faces the one he communes with, so he should not do anything impolite, especially if the other is great; thus the one praying is likened to someone facing a great person, so he should not do anything contrary to good manners. His statement: 'For on his right are two angels' - the agreed-upon hadith mentions 'angel' in singular; but it is problematic because on the left also is an angel; the answer is that it is reported in the hadith of Abu Umama: 'He stands before Allah, with an angel on his right and a companion on his left'; so if he spits to his left, it falls on his companion, which is the devil, and nothing of it hits the angel. As in al-Ayni on Bukhari and in the commentary of Ibn Hajar. Some excluded those in the Prophet's mosque facing the qiblah, because spitting to the right is better since the left is [in the direction of the grave?]. He said it is a sound view, as if there is a congregation on his left and he cannot put it under his foot, then spitting to the right is better. I say: especially if the praying person is in the Rawdah. His statement: 'In the Sahihayn' - it is argued that it indicates the permissibility of spitting in the mosque, because if it were a sin, it would not be expiated by burial alone but by repentance. The answer: repentance for every sin is known to be obligatory and is omitted; so the meaning of 'its expiation is to bury it' is along with repentance, as evidenced by calling it a sin. Ibn Amir Haj said.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya discusses the ruling on spitting during prayer. It is disliked (makruh) to spit in front or to the right; one should spit to the left or under the left foot if outside the mosque. Inside the mosque, spitting is a sin and its expiation is to bury the spit. The commentary explains that when praying, one is communing with Allah, so spitting is inappropriate. The hadith about the two angels is clarified: on the right is an angel recording good deeds, on the left is a companion (qarin) who is a devil; thus spitting to the left hits the devil. There is also an exception for the Prophet's mosque: if facing the qiblah, spitting to the right is better because the left side is towards the grave. The passage also addresses a potential objection: if spitting in the mosque is a sin, burial alone cannot expiate it; the answer is that burial is effective only with repentance.

Key Terms
البزاق (al-bazaq): Saliva expelled from the mouth, distinguished from 'reeq' (saliva inside mouth).
خطيئة (khati'ah): Sin; spitting in the mosque is classified as a sin requiring expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the expiation for spitting in the mosque?
A) Simply burying the spit
B) Burying the spit and making repentance
C) Performing two rak'ahs of prayer
D) Giving charity

Key Takeaway
Spitting in the mosque is a sin that requires both burial of the spit and sincere repentance; outside the mosque, one should spit to the left or under the foot.

Rulings on Iq'a', Naqr, and Ifritash of Arms
Prohibits iq'a', naqr, and ifritash in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iq'a', naqr, and ifritash, identify the proof from hadith, and distinguish the two interpretations of iq'a'.

Core Idea
Iq'a', naqr, and ifritash are prohibited postures in prayer; iq'a' is prohibitively disliked.

In this lesson, we examine three disliked actions in prayer: sitting in the iq'a' posture, performing ruku' and sujud hastily (naqr), and spreading the forearms on the ground during sujud (ifritash). The Hanafi school rules these as makruh tahrimi based on the prophetic prohibition. We also explore the two definitions of iq'a' and the scholarly refinement regarding its ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage explicitly mentions iq'a', naqr, and ifritash as disliked. Determine the level of dislike for each from the commentary (makruh tahrimi for iq'a').
Iq'a' is singled out as 'كراهة تحريم' (prohibitive dislike). Naqr and ifritash are also disliked, though the text does not specify the level as clearly.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Abu Hurairah, where the Prophet forbade these actions. Identify the exact text cited.
The hadith is an ahad hadith, providing zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'نهاني' (He forbade me) indicates prohibition. Analyze how this yields a ruling of makruh tahrimi in Hanafi usul, where a prohibition from a zanni source indicates karahah tahrimiyyah.
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition from an ahad hadith gives makruh tahrimi rather than haram, unless there is corroboration.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on iq'a'
Two definitions of iq'a' exist: one by Abu Hurairah (buttocks on ground, knees erect) and another by al-Karkhi (sitting on heels). Both are disliked, but the first is more accurate.
Al-Zayla'i and others prefer the first as it matches the description 'اقعاء الكلب'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon ruling is that all three actions are disliked, with iq'a' being prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi). The first interpretation of iq'a' is the primary definition, but the second is also disliked.
This is based on the text, with references to al-Fath and al-Mudmarat.

Commentary
This passage discusses three disliked actions in prayer: (1) Iq'a' (sitting on the buttocks with knees erect), which is prohibitively disliked; the commentary notes two interpretations, both disliked. (2) Naqr (pecking), meaning to perform ruku' and sujud quickly and lightly, imitating a rooster. (3) Ifritash dhira'ayn (spreading the forearms on the ground during prostration). All are disliked based on hadith from Abu Huraira and Aisha. The text ends by beginning the description of ifritash.

Key Terms
الإقعاء (al-iq'a'): Sitting on the buttocks, knees erect, hands on the ground, resembling a dog.
النقر (al-naqr): Performing ruku' and sujud hastily like a rooster pecking, without proper stillness.
افتراش الذراعين (ifritash al-dhira'ayn): Spreading the forearms flat on the ground during sujud, contrary to the sunnah of raising them.

Disliked Postures in Prayer: Al-Iq'a, Al-Naqr, and Al-Iftirash
Explains disliked sitting postures with Hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iq'a, naqr, and iftirash, distinguish between the two definitions of iq'a, and apply the rule that the prohibition indicates makruh tahrimi.

Core Idea
Three postures are prohibited in prayer: iq'a, naqr, and iftirash—all indicate lack of khushu'.

In this lesson, we examine three disliked actions in prayer as per the Hanafi school: iq'a (sitting on the buttocks with knees raised), naqr (hastening in ruku' and sujud), and iftirash al-dhira'ayn (spreading forearms on the ground in sujud). The commentator explains the definitions, references the hadith of Abu Huraira, and clarifies an intra-Hanafi difference on the meaning of iq'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked posture
List the three actions: iq'a, naqr, iftirash. Note their descriptions from the text.
Iq'a has two definitions; note both.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith of Abu Huraira that prohibits these acts. Understand its structure.
The hadith uses a prohibition (nahy) which in Hanafi usul indicates makruh tahrimi.
3
Apply Hanafi usul principle
Recognize that the prohibition (nahy) from the hadith makes these acts makruh tahrimi, not haram, because the dalil is zanni al-thubut.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on iq'a
Compare Al-Karkhi's definition (sitting on heels) with the primary one (buttocks on ground). Note Al-Zayla'i's tarjih and that both are disliked.
5
Apply the rulings to men and women
Iftirash is disliked for men but permissible for women. Iq'a and naqr apply to both genders.

His saying: (And iq'a is disliked) with a prohibition-level dislike. His saying: (And he sets up his knees) and brings them to his chest and places his hands on the ground. Al-Karkhi said it is to set up his feet and sit on his heels with his hands on the ground. Commentary
This passage discusses disliked postures in prayer, specifically al-iq'a (sitting on the buttocks with knees raised) and its definition. The commentator explains that it is makruh tahrimi. He notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Al-Karkhi defines it differently (sitting on heels), but Al-Zayla'i prefers the first definition as more like a dog's iq'a, though both are disliked. The ruling applies outside prayer as well on the first interpretation. The term 'the act of the devil' (uqbatu al-shaytan) refers to a specific posture, and the commentator gives its variant vowels. All these are disliked because they contradict khushu'.

Key Terms

افتراش ذراعيه (iftirāsh dhirā'ayhi): Spreading the forearms on the ground during sujud like a predator.

Tucking Sleeves (Tashmir): A Disliked Act of Arrogance
Tucking sleeves in prayer: ruling and reasoning from Hashiya al-Tahtawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for tucking sleeves in prayer, explain the reasoning of arrogance, and identify the disagreement when done before entering prayer.

Core Idea
Tucking sleeves in prayer shows pride; it is disliked unless done before prayer with a valid reason.

This lesson focuses on one of the disliked actions (makruhat) in prayer: tucking the sleeves above the forearms. While it seems minor, the Hanafi school teaches that such acts detract from the humility (khushu') that is the soul of prayer. We will explore the ruling, the reasoning, and a subtle disagreement among Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is tashmir? The text says it is disliked due to harshness (jafa').
2
Understand the reasoning
Some scholars attribute it to arrogance (takabbur), which contradicts the essence of prayer.
3
Consider the prior action
If sleeves were tucked before entering prayer, there is a difference of opinion on whether it is still disliked.
The text says 'there is difference of opinion on dislike'.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Most authorities advise against tucking during prayer; for tucking before, the safer view is to avoid but it is not majorly disliked.

Source Text

His saying: (And tucking his sleeves away from them) i.e., from his forearms, whether to the elbow or not according to the apparent, because the cloth covers all. If he tucked them before prayer then entered, there is difference of opinion on dislike. His saying: (because of harshness) some said because of arrogance which contradicts the essence of prayer. His saying: (And his prayer in sirwal or in izr) Al-Fath says: prayer in a wrap is not disliked, and in one garment not on his shoulder it is disliked except due to necessity. Izr is both masculine and feminine. His saying: (because of negligence) indicates prohibition-level dislike. His saying: (And a miqna'a) is a garment on the head tied under the chin. The qina' is wider, the khimar is larger. His saying: (There is no harm for the one praying to respond) Al-Hulwani said: no harm in speaking to the one praying and his responding with his head or hand; if one greets him, he replies in his mind according to Muhammad, and after prayer; Abu Yusuf says he does not reply at all. Al-Khattabi and al-Tahawi said the Prophet replied to Ibn Mas'ud after finishing prayer. His saying: (So the angels called him) i.e., the verse, but it is said that speech was allowed in earlier laws, so it is not applicable. His saying: without excuse; with excuse there is no dislike because necessity permits leaving wajib, so sunnah even more. His saying: (for leaving the sunnah of sitting) indicates it is tanzih-dislike. His saying: (And it is inserting the shins into the thighs) rather under the thighs. His saying: (And qas of his hair) is tying it on the nape or head.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage covers various disliked acts in prayer from Hashiya Al-Tahtawi. The first is tucking sleeves (tashmir), which is disliked because it shows arrogance or harshness, unless done before prayer in which case there is ikhtilaf. Next, praying in only a sirwal (trousers) or izr (waist-wrapper) is makruh tahrimi when one can wear a shirt, due to negligence. The proper clothing for men is three garments (izr, shirt, turban) and for women a shirt, khimar, and miqna'a. Responding to a salam with head or hand is allowed, but the verbal response differs between Abu Yusuf (no reply) and Muhammad (reply in mind and after prayer); the commentary favors Muhammad's view. Sitting tarbu' (cross-legged) without excuse is makruh tanzih because it leaves the sunnah of sitting. Finally, binding hair (aqs) on the nape is disliked. All these actions detract from khushu'.

Key Terms
تشمير كميه (tashmīr kummayhi): Tucking the sleeves above the forearms before or during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Haram

Tucking sleeves in prayer is disliked because it signifies arrogance; avoid it for better khushu'.

Praying in Minimal Clothing: The Dislike of the Sirwal/Izar
Ruling on praying in sirwal/izar from Hashiya al-Tahtawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for praying in only a sirwal or izar, explain why it is makruh tahrimi, and identify the proper attire for prayer.

Core Idea
Praying in only a sirwal shows negligence; it is prohibitedly disliked unless one has no other garment.

The Hanafi school emphasizes proper dress for prayer. This lesson covers the ruling on praying in a waist-wrapper (izar) or trousers (sirwal) without covering the upper body. It is considered a serious dislike (makruh tahrimi) due to carelessness. We will also touch on the ideal attire for men and women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Praying in only a sirwal or izar, i.e., not wearing a shirt or covering the shoulders.
2
State the ruling
The text says it is makruh due to negligence, which indicates prohibition-level dislike (makruh tahrimi).
3
Understand the reasoning
It shows carelessness (tahaawun) about the dignity of prayer.
4
Compare the ideal
The sunnah is to wear full garments: for men: shirt, izr, turban; for women: shirt, khimar, miqna'a.
5
Note exceptions
If one has no other garment, it is not disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتشمير كميه عنهما) أي عن ذراعيه سواء كان إلى المرفقين أو لا على الظاهر كما في البحر لصدق كف الثوب على الكل، ولو شمرهما قبل الصلاة، ثم دخل فيها اختلف في الكراهة كذا في النهر. قوله : (لما فيه من الجفاء) عبر بعضهم بقوله : لما فيه من التكبر المنافي لموضوع الصلاة اهـ. قوله : وصلاته في السراويل أو في إزار) قال في الفتح والصلاة متوشحاً لا تكره، وفي ثوب واحد ليس على عاتقه بعضه تكره إلا لضرورة العدم والإزار يذكر ويؤنث يقال : هو إزار، وهي أزار ومئزر وزن منبر مثله قوله لما فيه من التهاون) هذا يفيد كراهة التحريم. قوله: (ومقنعة) هي بكسر الميم وسكون القاف وفتح النون ثوب يوضع على الرأس ويربط تحت الحنك، والقناع أوسع منه لأنه يعطف من تحت الحنك، ويربط على القفا، والخمار أكبر منهما لأنه يغطي به الرأس، وترسل أطرافه على الظهر، أو الصدر قوله: (لا) بأس للمصلي أن يجيب) قال الحلواني : لا بأس أن يتكلم مع المصلي، وأن يجيب هو برأسه، أو بيده ولو سلم على المصلي يرد في نفسه عنده، وبعد الصلاة عند محمد، ولا يرد ، مطلقاً عند أبي يوسف اهـ وذكر الخطابي والطحاوي أن النبي رد على ابن مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه بعد فراغه من الصلاة كذا في الشرح عن مجمع الروايات وهو يؤيد قول محمد قوله: (فنادته الملائكة) أي لقوله تعالى: فنادته الخ وفيه أنه يمكن أن يقال: إن الكلام في الصلاة كان جائزاً في شريعتهم كما كان جائز في صدر الإسلام فحيث جاز نفس الكلام فالمناداة له من غيره أولى، فالأولى الإقتصار على الدليل الأول. قوله بلا عذر أما بالعذر فلا كراهة لأن العذر : يبيع ترك الواجب فأولى السنة. قوله لترك سنة (القعود هذا يفيد أنه مكروه تنزيهاً أفاده الشرح. قوله: (وهو إدخال الساقين في الفخذين الأولى تحت الفخذين كما ترشد إليه عبارته في الشرح. قوله : (وعقص شعره) وهو شدّه على القفا، أو الرأس لأنه صلى الله عليه وسلم مر برجل
His saying: (And tucking his sleeves away from them) i.e., from his forearms, whether to the elbow or not according to the apparent, because the cloth covers all. If he tucked them before prayer then entered, there is difference of opinion on dislike. His saying: (because of harshness) some said because of arrogance which contradicts the essence of prayer. His saying: (And his prayer in sirwal or in izr) Al-Fath says: prayer in a wrap is not disliked, and in one garment not on his shoulder it is disliked except due to necessity. Izr is both masculine and feminine. His saying: (because of negligence) indicates prohibition-level dislike. His saying: (And a miqna'a) is a garment on the head tied under the chin. The qina' is wider, the khimar is larger. His saying: (There is no harm for the one praying to respond) Al-Hulwani said: no harm in speaking to the one praying and his responding with his head or hand; if one greets him, he replies in his mind according to Muhammad, and after prayer; Abu Yusuf says he does not reply at all. Al-Khattabi and al-Tahawi said the Prophet replied to Ibn Mas'ud after finishing prayer. His saying: (So the angels called him) i.e., the verse, but it is said that speech was allowed in earlier laws, so it is not applicable. His saying: without excuse; with excuse there is no dislike because necessity permits leaving wajib, so sunnah even more. His saying: (for leaving the sunnah of sitting) indicates it is tanzih-dislike. His saying: (And it is inserting the shins into the thighs) rather under the thighs. His saying: (And qas of his hair) is tying it on the nape or head.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage covers various disliked acts in prayer from Hashiya Al-Tahtawi. The first is tucking sleeves (tashmir), which is disliked because it shows arrogance or harshness, unless done before prayer in which case there is ikhtilaf. Next, praying in only a sirwal (trousers) or izr (waist-wrapper) is makruh tahrimi when one can wear a shirt, due to negligence. The proper clothing for men is three garments (izr, shirt, turban) and for women a shirt, khimar, and miqna'a. Responding to a salam with head or hand is allowed, but the verbal response differs between Abu Yusuf (no reply) and Muhammad (reply in mind and after prayer); the commentary favors Muhammad's view. Sitting tarbu' (cross-legged) without excuse is makruh tanzih because it leaves the sunnah of sitting. Finally, binding hair (aqs) on the nape is disliked. All these actions detract from khushu'.

Key Terms
إزار (izār): A waist-wrapper, lower garment. Can be masculine or feminine.
سراويل (sirwāl): Trousers or pants worn by men.
تشمير كميه (tashmīr kummayhi): Tucking the sleeves above the forearms before or during prayer.
تربع (tarbu'): Cross-legged sitting in prayer, which is makruh tanzih if no excuse.
عقص ('aqs): Binding the hair on the nape or top of the head in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for praying in only a sirwal when a shirt is available?
A) Makruh tahrimi
B) Makruh tanzih
C) Mubah
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
Praying in minimal clothing when better clothes are available is makruh tahrimi; always dress properly for prayer.

Salah — Prayer

Responding to a Greeting during Prayer: Hanafi Rules and Disagreement
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on responding to salam in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on responding to a salutation during prayer and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
In prayer, one may respond to a salam with a gesture; verbal response is differed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but the relied-upon view is to reply after prayer.

Being greeted while praying is common. Is it allowed to respond? The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer: responding with a head or hand gesture is permissible, but how to handle the verbal response has an intra-school disagreement. This lesson dissects that ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Someone says salam to you while you are in prayer.
2
Know the permissibility
The text says 'there is no harm' in responding with head or hand gesture.
3
Understand the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf says no verbal response at all; Muhammad says respond in the mind and after prayer.
4
See the evidence
Hadith of Prophet responding to Ibn Mas'ud after prayer supports Muhammad.
5
Determine mufta bihi
The relied-upon is to gesture during prayer and reply verbally after.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how should one respond to a salutation during prayer?
A) Say 'Wa alaykum as-salam' immediately
B) Nod and reply after prayer
C) Do nothing
D) Wave hand

Key Takeaway
You may use a gesture to acknowledge a salam during prayer, but verbal reply must wait until after prayer.

Sitting Cross-Legged and Binding Hair in Prayer: Disliked Acts
Rulings on tarbu' and aqs from Hashiya al-Tahtawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for sitting tarbu' and binding hair (aqs) in prayer, and identify when the dislike is lifted.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged and tying hair in prayer are disliked because they deviate from the sunnah or involve forbidden imitation.

This lesson covers two more disliked actions: sitting cross-legged (tarbu') and tying hair on the nape (aqs). Both are considered makruh tanzih, meaning they are not forbidden but better avoided. Understanding these helps perfect one's prayer.

Guided Path
1
For tarbu': Identify the act and the sunnah
Sitting cross-legged is a deviation from the sunnah of iftirash (sitting on left foot, right foot erect).
2
State the ruling for tarbu'
It is makruh tanzih because it leaves the sunnah.
3
Identify the exception
If one has a valid excuse (e.g., injury), there is no dislike.
4
For aqs: Identify the act
Binding hair on the nape or top of the head is prohibited by the Prophet, thus disliked.
5
Understand the reasoning
Both actions involve either abandoning the prophetic practice or imitating prohibited behavior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Posture, Dress, and Gestures
Listing disliked actions with proofs from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the disliked actions in prayer according to the Hanafi school, citing the relevant proofs.

Core Idea
Preserving khushu' requires avoiding specific postures, dress, and gestures that the Prophet prohibited or disliked.

Prayer is an act of profound devotion; its perfection requires not only fulfilling obligations but also avoiding actions that undermine humility. In this passage, al-Tahtawi enumerates those disliked actions—from sitting postures to clothing choices—grounding each in prophetic example. Understanding these makruhat helps you refine your prayer and safeguard its spirit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disliked act
For each item mentioned, recognize the specific action being ruled upon (e.g., sitting like a dog, i'tijar, sadl).
2
Determine the ruling and its strength
Classify the action as makruh tahrimi or makruh tanzihi based on the evidence cited (e.g., explicit prohibition vs. general dislike).
Not all are equally disliked; some have stronger proof.
3
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the hadith or rationale the author gives for each ruling. For example, the hadith 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones...' for tucking up garment.
4
Understand the reason for dislike
Connect the action to the underlying principle: it contradicts khushu' (humility) or imitates arrogance, beasts, or disbelievers.
Example: folding sleeves shows harshness, against khushu'.
5
Identify exceptions and nuances
Note where the ruling changes (e.g., sadl is not disliked if done out of necessity such as extreme cold).
Exceptions preserve flexibility without violating the spirit.

May Allah be pleased with her: The Prophet used to forbid 'the sitting of Satan' and that a man spread his arms like a beast; al-Bukhari narrated it. 'Aqbat al-shaytan' is the buttocks. Folding his sleeves away from them (arms) is disliked because it contains harshness contrary to humility. His prayer in trousers or a lower garment while able to wear a shirt is disliked due to negligence and laziness and lack of etiquette. It is recommended for a man to pray in three garments: lower garment, shirt, and turban; and for a woman in a shirt, headcover, and chin-veil. Returning salutation by gesture is permissible because it is greeting in meaning. In al-Dhakhira: There is no harm for a praying person to answer a speaker with his head. And the narration from her: There is no harm in a man speaking to the praying person, so the angels called him while he was standing praying in the niche, the verse. Sitting cross-legged without excuse (disliked) because it abandons the sunna of sitting, but it is not disliked outside prayer because most of the Prophet's sitting was cross-legged, and likewise 'Umar ibn al-Khattab. And it is the insertion of the legs into the thighs so it becomes four. Plaiting his hair (disliked) which is tying it at the back of the neck or head because the Prophet passed by a man praying with his hair plaited and said: Let your hair prostrate with you. Also disliked is i'tijar: tying the head with a handkerchief or winding his turban on his head leaving its middle uncovered; and it is said: to cover his nose with his turban. Due to the Prophet's prohibition of i'tijar in prayer. Tucking up his garment (disliked) meaning raising it in front or behind when he wants to prostrate; and it is said: to gather his garment and tie it around his waist because it contains arrogance contrary to humility, due to his saying: I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to tuck up hair or garment; agreed upon. Also disliked is sadl out of arrogance and negligence, but with excuse it is not disliked. It is to place the garment on his head and shoulders or shoulders only, and let its sides hang without folding them, due to Abu Hurayra's statement that the Prophet forbade sadl. And that a man covers his mouth, so talthum (covering mouth) is disliked, and covering the nose and mouth.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah lists several disliked actions (makruhat) in prayer. The passage type is a collection of rulings with proofs (ruling_with_proof). The author first mentions the prohibition of sitting like a dog (u'qbat al-shaytan) and spreading arms like a beast, citing a hadith from al-Bukhari. Then he explains that folding sleeves away from the arms is disliked because it shows harshness incompatible with khushu'. Praying in trousers or a lower garment when one can wear a shirt is also disliked due to negligence. The recommended dress for men is three garments: izār, qamīṣ, and 'imāma; for women: qamīṣ, khimār, and miqna'a. Responding to salām with a gesture is allowed because the greeting is still conveyed in meaning; the text notes that in al-Dhakhira it is permissible to answer with the head. Sitting cross-legged without excuse is disliked as it abandons the sunna of sitting (iftirāsh), but not disliked outside prayer because the Prophet often sat cross-legged. Plaiting hair (ʿaqṣ) is disliked based on the hadith where the Prophet told a man to let his hair prostrate with him. Iʿtijār (wrapping the head with a cloth leaving the crown exposed) is also disliked due to explicit prohibition. Tucking up the garment (kaff al-thawb) is disliked because it resembles arrogance and contradicts the command to not tuck up hair or garment during prostration. Sadl (letting the garment hang loosely without wearing it properly) is disliked when done out of pride, but not if excused by extreme cold or heat. Finally, covering the mouth (talaththum) is disliked. Throughout, the author classifies these as makruh tahrimi or tanzihi based on the strength of the evidence, with most being makruh tanzihi unless explicitly prohibited. The commentary emphasizes that these rulings aim to preserve humility and proper adab in prayer.

Key Terms
عقبة الشيطان (ʿuqbat al-shayṭān): A sitting posture where a person sits on the buttocks with knees raised, resembling a dog or a shaytan, disliked in prayer.
الاعتجار (al-iʿtijār): Wrapping the head with a cloth or turban such that the crown is left uncovered, or covering the nose with the turban.
السدل (al-sadl): To let the garment hang loosely from the head and shoulders without wearing it properly, so that the hands are not inserted into sleeves.

Key Takeaway
Prayer's perfection requires avoiding even disliked actions that compromise khushu', as taught by the Prophet's practice and prohibitions.

Praying with Tied Hair and I'tijar: Rulings and Proof
Ruling with proof from hadith on disliked actions in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying with tied hair and i'tijar, and identify the proof and the degree of dislike.

Core Idea
Tying hair or wrapping head in prayer is disliked, not invalid — the hadith guides but does not void.

This lesson examines two common but disliked actions during prayer. The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith, considers both tying the hair and a specific head wrap (i'tijar) as makruh tahrimi. Understanding the difference between disliked and invalid is crucial for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Ma'qus al-sha'r (tied hair) and i'tijar (wrapping head with cloth/turban leaving middle exposed or covering nose).
Both are separate but share the same ruling.
2
Note the ruling
Both are makruh (disliked) — specifically makruh tahrimi according to the commentary.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Examine the proof
The hadith 'دع شعرك يسجد معك' commands letting hair prostrate, implying it should not be bound. The separate prohibition of i'tijar strengthens the dislike.
The dalil is a hadith ahad, hence zanni al-thubut, yielding dislike rather than prohibition.
4
Distinguish dislike from invalidation
The prayer is valid but disliked; invalidation only occurs if excessive action (amal kathir) is committed, such as tying hair during the prayer.
The intention does not change the ruling: whether done before or during prayer — if before, disliked if done intentionally; if during, invalidates.
5
Consider nuances
Braiding and leaving hair loose is not prohibited. I'tijar has two definitions: winding turban leaving middle exposed, or covering nose with turban.
The differing definitions are mentioned in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith 'Let your hair prostrate with you.' The commentary clarifies that praying with hair tied up is disliked (makruh). It is disliked whether one tied it before prayer intentionally or not. The definition of i'tijar is wrapping the head with a cloth or turban leaving the middle exposed, or covering the nose with the turban. The evidence is the Prophet's prohibition, which indicates it is makruh tahrimi. The commentary also explains that if the hair is braided and left loose, it is not prohibited. The key point is that the prayer itself is valid but disliked; only excessive action (like tying during prayer) would invalidate it.

Key Terms

الاعتجار (al-i'tijar): Wrapping the head with a cloth or turban leaving the middle exposed, or covering the nose with the turban

Ruling on Isdal Outside Prayer: When Does Dislike Arise?
Clarifying that isdal is not disliked outside prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on isdal outside prayer and identify the conditions that make it disliked.

Core Idea
Isdal outside prayer is not disliked unless arrogance is present.

This lesson clarifies a common misconception about isdal (letting the garment hang loosely) outside of prayer. While isdal during prayer has specific rulings, outside prayer the default is permissibility. However, the Hanafi school adds a nuance: if arrogance is involved, it becomes disliked even outside prayer. The commentary by Ibn Amir Haj refines this further by considering the presence or absence of an excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is isdal (letting the garment hang loosely) outside prayer. The text states the default ruling: there is no dislike (la karaha) according to the correct position.
This default applies only under certain conditions.
2
Note the condition from Ibn Amir Haj
Ibn Amir Haj qualifies the default: it applies only when there is no excuse (udhr) and no arrogance (takabbur). This introduces conditional factors.
3
Analyze the first scenario: excuse without arrogance
If there is a valid excuse (e.g., cold, physical condition) and no arrogance, then there is no dislike absolutely. The default ruling holds.
Excuse alone does not trigger dislike.
4
Analyze the second scenario: arrogance present
If there is arrogance, either with or without an excuse, then isdal is disliked absolutely. Arrogance is the decisive factor.
The presence of arrogance overrides any excuse.
5
Conclude the refined ruling
Thus, isdal outside prayer is permissible unless done with arrogance. Intention (niyyah) and circumstance (excuse) determine the ruling.
This refinement aligns with the Hanafi principle that actions are judged by intentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا كراهة في السدل خارج الصلاة على الصحيح قال ابن أمير حاج، في السدل : هذا كله عند عدم العذر وعدم التكبر فإن كان لعذر من غير تكبر فلا كراهة مطلقاً، العذر متكبراً، أو للتكبر فقط كره مطلقاً .اهـ.
There is no dislike in isdal outside prayer according to the correct position. Ibn Amir Haj said regarding isdal: All this is when there is no excuse and no arrogance. If it is due to an excuse without arrogance, then there is no dislike absolutely. If there is an excuse with arrogance, or arrogance alone, then it is disliked absolutely.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the hashiya clarifying the base text which states that isdal (letting the garment hang loosely without tucking) is not disliked outside prayer. The commentator, Ibn Amir Haj, refines this by adding conditions: dislike only arises when there is arrogance or when it is done without excuse. If there is a valid excuse (e.g., cold) and no arrogance, there is no dislike. If arrogance is present, it is disliked even outside prayer. This is a typical Hanafi refinement that considers intention and circumstance.

Key Terms
السدل (al-sadl): Letting the garment hang loosely from the shoulders without tucking or fastening
كراهة (karaha): Dislike, a legal classification between permissible and prohibited; may be tanzihi (lightly disliked) or tahrimi (prohibitively disliked)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When done with arrogance
C) When done with an excuse
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Isdal outside prayer is not disliked by default; it becomes disliked only when arrogance is present, regardless of any excuse.

Dislike of Wrapping Garment Like Samma'
Prohibition of wrapping the garment without leaving an opening for hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on al-ishtimala al-samma' and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
Wrapping without hand openings is makruh tanzihi due to resemblance to Jewish practice.

This lesson examines a specific disliked manner of wrapping the garment during prayer, known as al-ishtimala al-samma'. The source text cites a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that prohibits imitating the Jews in this practice. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish between what is merely disliked and what invalidates the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wrapping oneself in a garment without leaving an opening for the hands. The Hanafi ruling is makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked).
The act does not invalidate prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith: 'If one of you has two garments, let him pray in them...'
The hadith is ahad, with zanni thubut and dalalah.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition 'do not wrap like the Jews' indicates dislike (karahah) per Hanafi usul, not absolute prohibition.
The negative command does not necessarily imply haram; context matters.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) classify it as haram due to the explicit prohibition.
The practical difference is the degree of sin, but both agree the prayer is valid.
5
Evaluate the ruling level
Hanafis rule it makruh tanzihi because the prohibition is not decisive enough to render the act haram.
The evidence of resemblance to non-Muslims is a secondary consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( و ) يكره الاندراج فيه أي الثوب بحيث لا يدع منفذاً (يخرج يديه) منه، وهي الاشتمالة الصماء قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إذا كان لأحدكم ثويان فليصل فيهما فإن لم يكن له إلا ثوب فليتزر به ولا يشتمل اشتمالة اليهود
And it is disliked to wrap oneself in the garment such that one does not leave an opening to take out one's hands from it. This is the ishtimala al-samma'. Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The base text states that it is disliked to wrap the garment around the body without leaving an opening for the hands, a practice called ishtimala al-samma'. The hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibits imitating the Jews in their manner of wrapping. The Hanafi school classifies this as makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) because it resembles the practice of non-Muslims but does not invalidate the prayer. The hadith is cited as the primary evidence.

Key Terms
الاشتمالة الصماء (al-ishtimala al-samma'): Wrapping the entire body with a single garment without leaving any opening for the hands, resembling a Jewish practice

The Hanafi ruling on ishtimala al-samma' is makruh tanzihi; the prayer is valid but the act is disliked.
